â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *Write the answer **in** English, **with** a black pen*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **b) Manner/Quality Prepositions** (in what manner)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *with*: *She answered **with** confidence | He spoke **with** anger*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *in*: *She spoke **in** whispers | Dance **in** rhythm*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *like*: *She behaves **like** a professional* (resemblance/manner)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *He greeted her **with** warmth | She ran **in** panic*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *They danced **like** professionals* (manner/degree of resemblance)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **c) Accompaniment Prepositions** (with whom)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *with*: *I went **with** my sister | Come **with** us*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *together with*: *Together **with** his friends, he celebrated*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Position**: Usually end-of-clause
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Verb agreement**: *She **with** her friends **was** late* (agree with main subject "she")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **d) Agent Prepositions** (Passive voice)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *by*: *The book was written **by** Shakespeare* (agent of passive verb)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *Position**: Always follows passive verb
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Examples**: *The disease was spread **by** contact* | *The poem was memorized **by** students*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **5. Cause/Reason Prepositions** (WHY)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Definition**: Express causation, reason, or motivation
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Causative Prepositions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *because of*: *We left **because of** the rain* (cause/reason)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *due to*: *The delay was **due to** traffic* (more formal; cause)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *on account of*: ***On account of** his illness, he stayed home* (formal; reason)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Distinction**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *because of* most common, neutral
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *due to* somewhat formal; often used with "be"
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *on account of* very formal/archaic
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *She quit **because of** low salary*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *The event was cancelled **due to** weather*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **b) Motivation Prepositions** (purpose)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *for*: *I did it **for** her | He studies **for** exams* (aim/purpose)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *out of*: *He helped **out of** kindness* (motivated by feeling)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚           â”œâ”€â”€ *They saved money **for** retirement*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚           â”œâ”€â”€ *She spoke **out of** concern for the community*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **6. Purpose/Benefit Prepositions** (FOR WHOM/WHAT)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Definition**: Indicate intended recipient, beneficiary, or purpose
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Recipient/Beneficiary Prepositions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *for*: *This gift is **for** you | I cooked **for** my family*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *to*: *Give this letter **to** her | I spoke **to** the manager*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Distinction**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *for* = intended purpose/recipient (emphasis on benefit)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *to* = direction/indirect object (emphasis on direction)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *He bought flowers **for** his mother* (for her benefit)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *He gave flowers **to** the receptionist* (direction of action)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **b) Purpose Infinitive** (intended goal)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *for*: *I went there **for** to study* ~ (archaic/dialectal)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Modern usage**: Infinitive alone usually preferred (*I went there to study*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Note**: *in order to*, *so as to* are alternative forms
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **7. Possession/Attribution Prepositions** (BELONGING TO)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Definition**: Express ownership, relationship, or characteristic belonging
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Possession Prepositions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *of*: *The book **of** John* (belongs to John; more formal than possessive 's)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Modern usage**: Possessive 's preferred (*John's book* > *the book of John*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Literary/Formal**: *the work of Shakespeare, the power of love*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Rule**: *of* often used with proper nouns in fixed expressions
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **b) Part-Whole Relationships**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *of*: *the leg **of** the table, the roof **of** the house*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Structure**: Whole + of + Part (emphasis on composition)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Examples**: *the streets of Rome, the pages of a book*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **8. Concerning/Reference Prepositions** (ABOUT)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Definition**: Indicate what something is about or references
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Topic/Subject Matter Prepositions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *about*: *a book **about** history, speak **about** politics*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *on*: *a lecture **on** biology, information **on** climate change* (more formal/academic)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *concerning*: *concerning your request, as **concerning** your application* (formal)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Distinction**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *about* most common, neutral
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *on* more formal/academic
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *concerning* formal/administrative
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *She asked **about** my health | They debated **on** economics*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **b) Opinion/Stance Prepositions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *about/on*: *What do you think **about/on** this idea?*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Variation**: Regional preference (AmE *about* > BrE *on*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **9. Comparison/Quantity Prepositions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Comparison Prepositions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *like*: *She looks **like** her mother* (resemblance)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *as*: *He works **as** a consultant* (in role of)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Distinction**: *like* for resemblance; *as* for role/function
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Examples**: *She sings **like** an angel | He acts **as** manager*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **b) Quantity/Amount Prepositions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚           â”œâ”€â”€ *of*: *a lot **of** people, a cup **of** coffee* (quantity specification)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚           â”œâ”€â”€ *among*: *one **among** many, chosen **among** thousands*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚           â””â”€â”€ **Usage**: Indicates partitive relationship (part of a whole)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **B. Morphological Classification (Form-Based)**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **1. Simple Prepositions** (single morpheme)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Single-word prepositions**: in, on, at, to, from, by, for, with, of, about, above, below, between, during, before, after
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Most common English prepositions**: Generally monosyllabic or very high frequency
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Characteristics**: Cannot be decomposed into smaller meaningful parts
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **2. Complex/Compound Prepositions** (multiple morphemes)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Definition**: Prepositions formed from combinations of words
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Noun + Preposition Combinations**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *in front of, in back of, in the middle of, in case of*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *by means of, by way of, at the end of*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *out of, because of, in spite of, regardless of*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Structure**: Preposition + Noun + Preposition OR Noun + Preposition
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **b) Verb + Preposition Combinations** (Participle forms)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *according to, considering, following*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *excepting, including, excluding*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Form**: Usually present participle (-ing form) or past participle
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **c) Adjective + Preposition Combinations**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *ahead of, apart from, due to, next to*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *opposite to, prior to, similar to, subsequent to*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Usage**: More formal or emphatic than simple alternatives
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **d) Adverb + Preposition Combinations**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *up to, on top of, along with*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Function**: Often convey spatial or temporal meanings
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Productivity**: Complex prepositions less productive than simple ones; mostly fixed expressions
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **3. Stranded/Hanging Prepositions** (Preposition at End of Clause)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Definition**: Prepositions appearing at end of sentence/clause instead of before complement
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Prescriptivist debate**: Traditionally discouraged ("Never end sentence with preposition!")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Modern acceptance**: Acceptable in most contexts, especially with relative clauses and questions
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) In Relative Clauses**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *The person I'm thinking **of** is my mentor.* âœ“ (stranded preposition)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *The person **of** whom I'm thinking is my mentor.* (pied-piping; formal)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Acceptability**: Stranded form more common in modern English
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Example alternatives**: *The project I'm working **on**... | The friend I relied **on**...*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **b) In Questions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *What are you talking **about**?* âœ“ (natural, stranded)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ ***About** what are you talking?* (formal/archaic pied-piping)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Which person did you give the book **to**?* âœ“
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *To whom did you give the book?* (formal/prescriptivist)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **c) In Passive Constructions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *This is an issue that must be dealt **with**.* âœ“
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *This is an issue **with** which something must be dealt* (archaic pied-piping)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Register**: Stranded form standard in contemporary English
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **d) With Infinitives**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *This is someone to look **up** to.* âœ“ (stranded)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *This is someone up to whom to look.* (pied-piping; very formal/rare)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Modern convention**: Stranded prepositions completely standard
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **4. Prepositions in Phrasal Verbs** (Particle function)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Definition**: Prepositions acting as particles in phrasal verbs (up, down, in, out, on, off, etc.)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Particle distinction**: When part of phrasal verb, sometimes called "particles" not prepositions
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Examples**: *give up, put off, run into, look after, bring about*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Interchangeability**: Particle position varies (*look it up* vs *look up it* â€” only first is standard)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Note**: Will be covered in more detail under Phrasal Verbs section
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **C. Syntactic Classification (Position & Function)**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **1. Prepositions in Prepositional Phrases (PP)**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Structure**: Preposition + Noun Phrase (object of preposition)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Head**: Preposition is the head of the phrase
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Function**: Can modify verbs (adverbial PPs) or nouns (adjectival PPs)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Adverbial PPs** (modify verbs/predicates)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He walked **in the park** yesterday.* (where and when did he walk)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She spoke **with confidence** about the topic.* (how did she speak)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *They left **because of** the rain.* (why did they leave)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Position**: Usually end-of-clause (post-verbal) or initial (marked/emphatic)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Multiple adverbial PPs**: Can stack at end (*in the park, on Tuesday, with friends*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **b) Adjectival PPs** (modify nouns)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *A book **on physics** is useful.* (modifies "book")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *The person **in the corner** is my friend.* (modifies "person")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *The girl **with red hair** arrived.* (modifies "girl")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Position**: Immediately after noun (post-nominal), right-branching
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Stacking**: Multiple PPs can modify same noun (*the book on physics in the library*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **c) Predicative PPs** (follow linking verbs)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *The keys are **in the drawer**.* (locational predicate)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She is **from Spain**.* (origin predicate)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He seems **in a good mood**.* (state predicate)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Function**: Describe or specify subject of linking verb
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **d) Argument PPs** (required complements)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *She is interested **in music**.* ("in music" required by "interested")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *He depends **on** his parents.* ("on" required by "depends")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *We agreed **on** the plan.* ("on" required by "agreed")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Distinction**: Not optional; verb/adjective subcategorizes for the PP
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **2. Prepositions as Functional Elements**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Definition**: Prepositions marking case relationships and semantic roles
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Case Marking Function**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Nominative case*: Subject (implicit; no preposition)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Accusative case*: Direct object (implicit; no preposition)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Oblique case*: Marked by prepositions
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Example**: *She gave the book to him* (to = marks oblique/dative case)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Function**: Prepositions in modern English express grammatical relations otherwise marked by inflections
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **b) Semantic Role Marking**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Agent/Actor*: usually unmarked (subject) OR *by* in passive
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Patient/Theme*: usually unmarked (direct object) OR *by* in passive
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Experiencer*: *to* (*interested to him* ~ archaic)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Instrument*: *with* (*cut with scissors*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Beneficiary*: *for* (*cooked for her*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Location*: *in, at, on* (various)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Time*: *in, on, at, during* (various)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Function**: Prepositions express argument structure relationships
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **c) Subcategorization with Prepositions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Verbs subcategorizing for specific prepositions**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *depend* + *on* (*depend on help*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *agree* + *with/on* (*agree with person* vs *agree on topic*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *listen* + *to* (*listen to music*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *care* + *about* (*care about others*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Adjectives subcategorizing for specific prepositions**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *interested* + *in* (*interested in sports*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *similar* + *to* (*similar to mine*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *proud* + *of* (*proud of her*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *aware* + *of* (*aware of the danger*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Key principle**: Preposition choice is lexically determined (arbitrary/fixed)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **3. Prepositions in Different Syntactic Positions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚ **Position flexibility**: Limited compared to adverbs
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Pre-nominal Position** (rare; only in idioms/frozen expressions)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *down payment* (preposition as modifying element; non-standard syntactically)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Rarity**: Prepositions typically don't appear before nouns in standard structures
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Exceptions**: Some fossilized expressions (*above-board*, but these become compounds/adjectives)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **b) Post-verbal Position** (adverbial function)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She walked in the park* (PP after verb)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Standard position for adverbial PPs**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Multiple PPs**: Can stack (*in the park, on Saturday, with friends*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **c) Post-nominal Position** (adjectival function)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *The book on the table* (PP modifies noun "book")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Obligatory position for adjectival PPs**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Cannot move**: *The on-the-table book* âœ—
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **d) Stranded Position** (end of clause)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚           â”œâ”€â”€ *What are you thinking about?* (preposition at clause end)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚           â”œâ”€â”€ *He's the kind of person I've been looking for* (stranded at end of relative clause)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚           â””â”€â”€ **Modern acceptability**: Completely standard in contemporary English
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **D. Preposition + Object Relationships**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **1. Object of Preposition Forms**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Definition**: Noun phrase immediately following preposition
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Case**: Oblique/objective case (in English, evident with pronouns)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Pronoun Objects**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Objective case required**: *to him* (âœ— *to he*), *about her* (âœ— *about she*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **First person**: *for me* (âœ— *for I*), *with us* (âœ— *with we*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Relative pronouns**: *about which* (âœ— *about who*), *to whom* (âœ— *to who*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Common error**: Using nominative *who* after prepositions (*Give it to who?* âœ— â†’ *Give it to whom?* âœ“)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **b) Noun Phrase Objects**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *in the morning, on Monday, at the party, under the table*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Complexity**: NP can include determiners, adjectives, and embedded PPs
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Example**: *in the beautiful old house in the countryside*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Structure**: Preposition + [Det + Adj* + N + PP*]
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **c) Gerund/Nominal Objects**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *before leaving, after eating, instead of waiting*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Gerund characteristics**: Verbal noun; takes objects (*before seeing him*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **With possessive**: *before his leaving* (formal) vs *before him leaving* (informal)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Examples**: *interested in painting, capable of learning, skilled at dancing*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **d) Clause Objects** (rare; usually marked by subordinator)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *She wasn't concerned about how others felt* (clause as prepositional object)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Structural note**: Typically requires a wh-clause or if-clause
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Examples**: *Pay attention to what I'm saying | Interest in whether it will work*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **2. Preposition Stranding in Object Position**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Question formation**: *What are you talking about?* (about = stranded)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Relative clauses**: *The person I'm thinking of* (of = stranded)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Passive voice**: *This must be dealt with* (with = stranded)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Modern convention**: Stranding completely acceptable in contemporary English
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **3. Pied-Piping** (Moving preposition with object)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Formal alternative to stranding**: *About which are you thinking?*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Structure**: Preposition + wh-phrase moves to clause-initial position
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Register**: Formal/academic register; increasingly archaic
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *To whom did you speak?* (formal; stranded: *Who did you speak to?*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Of which are you fond?* (archaic; stranded: *Which are you fond of?*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *During which period did this occur?* (formal; stranded: *Which period did this occur during?*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Modern norm**: Stranding strongly preferred over pied-piping in contemporary English
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **E. Exam Strategy & Key Testing Points**
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **1. Spatial Prepositions: in/on/at**
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *I'm at home. I'm on home.* | âœ“ *I'm **at** home* (location)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *The book is in the table.* | âœ“ *The book is **on** the table* (surface)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *He arrived in Rome.* | âœ“ *He arrived **in** Rome / *arrived **at** the station*
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Rule**: *at* (specific point), *in* (enclosed), *on* (surface)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **2. Temporal Prepositions: at/in/on**
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *at Monday* | âœ“ ***on** Monday* (specific day)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *in 3pm* | âœ“ ***at** 3pm* (specific time)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *on summer* | âœ“ ***in** summer* (season/month/year)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Strategy**: Memorize temporal uses: *at* for clock times, *on* for days, *in* for longer periods
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **3. Directional: to/toward/into**
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *She goes to swimming* | âœ“ *She goes **to** the pool / **swimming** (no preposition)*
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *Moving **toward** the exit | Moving **into** the room* (different meanings)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *toward* = direction (may not reach), *into* = entry (reaches destination)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Common error**: Confusing *to/into/toward* in movement contexts
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **4. Instrumental: with/by**
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *Cut **with** scissors* (direct instrument) | *Travel **by** car* (method/mode)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *I cut the bread by knife* | âœ“ *I cut the bread **with** a knife*
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Passive agent**: *Written **by** Shakespeare* (agent of passive verb)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Rule**: *with* for tools/direct means, *by* for method/agent
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **5. Cause: because of / due to**
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *We left **because of** the rain* (neutral cause)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *The delay was **due to** traffic* (formal; used with be-verb)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *Due to heavy rain, we stayed home.* ~ (controversial as sentence opener; now increasingly accepted)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Modern usage note**: *Due to* sentence-initial increasingly accepted; was prescriptively restricted to predicative position
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **6. Stranded Prepositions (Modern acceptability)**
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *What are you thinking **about**?* (natural, stranded)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *The person I was thinking **of*** (standard in modern English)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— (Prescriptivist) *"Never end a sentence with a preposition"* (outdated rule)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Exam focus**: Modern English accepts stranded prepositions; formal writing may prefer pied-piping
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **7. Preposition Omission Errors**
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *He is interested sports* | âœ“ *He is interested **in** sports*
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *I depend my parents* | âœ“ *I depend **on** my parents*
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *They agreed the plan* | âœ“ *They agreed **on** the plan*
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Strategy**: Learn which verbs/adjectives require specific prepositions (lexical dependency)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **8. Preposition + Object Case (Pronoun Objects)**
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *between you and I* | âœ“ *between you and **me*** (objective case after preposition)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *Give it to they* | âœ“ *Give it **to** them* (objective pronouns)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *She went with he* | âœ“ *She went **with** him*
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Rule**: All pronouns after prepositions must be objective case
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **9. Distinguish Similar Prepositions**
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *beside* (spatial: at the side) vs *besides* (meaning: except/moreover) â€” *besides* is adverb/conjunction
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *among* (3+ entities, distributed) vs *between* (2 entities or pairwise)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *in* (enclosed/interior) vs *on* (surface contact) vs *at* (point location)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Exam tip**: Context determines which similar preposition is appropriate
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **10. Phrasal Verb Preposition/Particle Placement**
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *look it up* âœ“ | *look up it* âœ— (particle after object for transitive)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *put the fire out* âœ“ | *put out the fire* âœ“ (both acceptable for some phrasal verbs)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *look up* âœ“ (intransitive; no object after particle)
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Rule**: Particles in transitive phrasal verbs must typically precede object or allow both positions
â”‚       â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **11. Compound Prepositions Recognition**
â”‚       â”‚           â”œâ”€â”€ *because of, in front of, regardless of, by means of, instead of*
â”‚       â”‚           â”œâ”€â”€ **Test**: If removing a part breaks meaning, it's compound
â”‚       â”‚           â”œâ”€â”€ *in front* (no meaning alone), **of** (no meaning alone) â†’ **compound preposition**
â”‚       â”‚           â””â”€â”€ **Strategy**: Memorize common compound prepositions for recognition in exam contexts
â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **4. Conjunction**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **A. Coordinating Conjunctions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Definition**: Words that join grammatically equal elements (words, phrases, clauses)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Function**: Create coordinate structures; join independent clauses or equal-level constituents
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Number**: Small fixed set (7 primary: and, but, or, nor, yet, so, for)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Abbreviation**: FANBOYS (For, And, Nor, But, Or, Yet, So)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **1. Additive Conjunctions** (joining/addition)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) AND**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Function**: Join similar ideas, elements, or clauses
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Word level**: *apples **and** oranges* (coordinate nouns)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Phrase level**: *quickly **and** quietly* (coordinate adverbs)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Clause level**: *She studied hard, **and** she passed the exam.* (coordinate independent clauses)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Additive; signals inclusion of both elements
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Nuances**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *logical sequence*: *He graduated, **and** he moved to New York* (implies sequence)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *temporal continuity*: *I worked all day, **and** I was exhausted* (and then)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *causal relation* (rare): *He ate too much, **and** he got sick* (suggests cause-effect)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Comma usage**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Compound sentences*: Comma before **and** when joining independent clauses
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She ran fast, **and** he walked slowly* âœ“ (comma required)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *She ran fast **and** he walked slowly* âœ— (missing comma)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *Series/Lists*: Serial comma (Oxford comma) before final **and**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *apples, oranges, **and** bananas* (with Oxford comma)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *apples, oranges **and** bananas* (without Oxford comma; British style)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Common usage patterns**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ Coordinate multiple similar elements
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ Join independent clauses (with comma)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ Create emphasis through conjunction (*going and going and going*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **b) NOR**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Function**: Join negative alternatives; "and not"
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Usage**: Neither one thing nor another (both negated)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Pattern**: Appears after negative element (*not...nor*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He doesn't like coffee, **nor** does he like tea.* (both disliked)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She is not happy, **nor** is she satisfied.* (neither state is true)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Inversion requirement** (formal):
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He doesn't swim, **nor** does he run.* âœ“ (inversion of auxiliary)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He doesn't swim, **nor** he runs.* âœ— (missing inversion)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Frequency**: Less common than "and" or "or"; more formal register
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **2. Adversative/Contrastive Conjunctions** (but/yet - contrast/exception)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) BUT**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Function**: Join ideas in contrast; signal exception to expectation
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: However; on the contrary; contrary to expectation
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Word/phrase level**: *smart **but** lazy* (contrasting adjectives)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Clause level**: *She studied hard, **but** she failed the exam.* (unexpected result)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Common pattern**: Positive statement â†’ **but** â†’ negative consequence
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He is intelligent, **but** he is lazy.*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *The plan sounds good, **but** it won't work.*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Comma usage**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ Independent clauses: comma required (*She ran, **but** he walked*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ Phrases/words: comma optional (*smart **but** lazy* or *smart, **but** lazy*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Emphasis variations**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *...but* (at sentence end for emphasis): *That's not just big, it's HUGE, but* âœ— (incorrect)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ Typically used mid-position
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Synonyms**: however, yet, nonetheless (but more formal)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **b) YET**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Function**: Signal contrast/surprise; "but" with more emphasis on contrast
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Despite this; and yet; still; however
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Usage**: More formal/literary than "but"; emphasizes paradox
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He worked hard, **yet** he remained poor.* (paradoxical)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She was sad, **yet** she smiled.* (contrasting states)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *The plan is simple, **yet** effective.* (unexpected combination)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Adverbial function** (temporal):
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Not finished **yet***  (still not completed)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *Have you arrived **yet**?* (temporal "still")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Distinction**: *but* = simple contrast; *yet* = paradoxical/surprising contrast
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **3. Alternative/Disjunctive Conjunctions** (or - choice/alternatives)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) OR**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Function**: Present alternatives or options
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: One or the other (or both, depending on context)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Types**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Inclusive or**: One, the other, or both (*You can have coffee **or** tea **or** both*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Exclusive or**: One or the other, but not both (*You're either coming **or** staying home*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Word level**: *apples **or** oranges* (choose one)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Clause level**: *She will study, **or** she will fail.* (alternative outcomes)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Multiple options**: *red **or** blue **or** green* (three alternatives)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Comma usage**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ Independent clauses: comma required (*Come now, **or** you'll be late*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ Short phrases: comma often omitted (*coffee **or** tea*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Singular vs. Plural considerations**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Cats **or** dogs are common pets.* (verb plural; plural element determines agreement)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *A cat **or** a dog is common.* (verb singular; singular element closer determines)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Rule**: Verb agrees with closer subject (if subjects differ in number)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Synonyms**: otherwise (implies consequence if not chosen)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **b) Alternative expressions**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *either...or* (emphatic alternative): Covered under Correlative Conjunctions
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *and/or* (legal/technical): Indicates inclusive or
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ *otherwise*: Strong alternative with consequence (*Do this, otherwise fail*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **4. Causative/Resultative Conjunctions** (so, for - reason/result)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) SO**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Function**: Introduce result/consequence of first clause
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Therefore; as a result; and that's why
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Pattern**: Cause â†’ **so** â†’ Result
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He studied hard, **so** he passed.* (study caused passing)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *It was raining, **so** we stayed inside.* (rain caused staying in)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Comma requirement**: Comma before **so** when joining independent clauses âœ“
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She was tired, **so** she rested.* âœ“
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She was tired **so** she rested.* âœ— (missing comma)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Degree modifier meaning** (separate from conjunction):
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She was **so** tired.* (intensifier, not conjunction)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *It was **so** good that...* (intensifier + that-clause)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Formal alternatives**: therefore, thus, consequently (more academic)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **b) FOR**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Function**: Introduce reason/explanation for preceding clause
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Because; for the reason that; that is
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Usage**: Formally joins independent clauses with reason relationship
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Pattern**: Main clause â†’ **for** â†’ Reason/explanation
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He must be here, **for** I saw his car.* (explanation)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She didn't come, **for** she was sick.* (reason)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Formality**: More formal/literary; less common in modern English
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Comma requirement**: Always required before **for** when conjoining clauses
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Modern alternatives**: because (preferred), since, as
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **5. Punctuation & Placement Rules (Coordinating Conjunctions)**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Comma Splice Prevention**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *She ran fast she won the race.* (comma splice)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *She ran fast, **and** she won the race.* (correct)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Rule**: Conjunction required when joining independent clauses with comma
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Note**: Without conjunction, use semicolon (*She ran fast; she won the race*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **b) Coordinate structure parallelism**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *She likes to read **and** playing games.* (infinitive + gerund)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *She likes to read **and** to play games.* (parallel infinitives)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Rule**: Coordinated elements must have same grammatical form
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Common error**: Mixing verb forms, noun types, or clause types
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **c) Starting sentences with coordinating conjunctions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *Formal writing*: Traditionally avoided
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *Modern usage*: Increasingly accepted for stylistic effect
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *And then she realized the truth.* (acceptable now)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *But that's not all.* (acceptable in many contexts)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Note**: Context and register determine appropriateness
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **6. Exam Strategy for Coordinating Conjunctions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Identify the relationship**: Additive (and), Contrastive (but/yet), Alternative (or), Resultative (so), Explanatory (for)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Comma placement**: Always use comma with coordinating conjunction between independent clauses
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Parallel structure**: Ensure coordinated elements are grammatically equal
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Common error**: Mixing voice (active + passive), tenses, or grammatical categories
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Subject-verb agreement**: With *or/nor*, verb agrees with nearest subject
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **B. Correlative Conjunctions** (Paired Conjunctions)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Definition**: Paired conjunctions used together to link equal grammatical elements
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Function**: Emphasize relationship; create more formal/emphatic structures
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Characteristic**: Always appear in pairs; both parts required for complete meaning
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Difference from coordinating**: More emphatic; formal register
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **1. either...or** (choice between two alternatives, exclusive or inclusive)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Function**: Present two mutually exclusive (or inclusive) options
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Structure**: **either** [option 1] **or** [option 2]
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Either you leave now, **or** I will call the police.* (exclusive)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *You can have **either** coffee **or** tea.* (choice)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *Either she'll arrive early, **or** she'll be late.* (binary outcome)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning nuances**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Inclusive usage*: Both options possible (*You can take either path*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *Exclusive usage*: Only one option (*Either attend or leave*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Parallel structure requirement**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *Either you work hard **or** being lazy* (verb form mismatch)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *Either you work hard **or** you are lazy* (parallel verb forms)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Subject-verb agreement**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Either the cat **or** the dog is missing.* (verb singular; closer subject singular)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Either the cats **or** the dog is missing.* (verb agrees with closer subject: singular "dog")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Rule**: Verb agrees with nearest subject
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Position**: Often at beginning of first option; both parts included
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **2. neither...nor** (both alternatives negated)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Function**: Negate both options; "not one and not the other"
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Structure**: **neither** [option 1] **nor** [option 2]
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Neither the cat **nor** the dog was found.* (both missing)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He is neither smart **nor** hardworking.* (lacks both qualities)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *Neither smoking **nor** drinking is allowed here.* (both prohibited)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Negation scope**: Negative meaning automatically conveyed
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *Neither the cat is not nor the dog is not here* (double negation, incorrect)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *Neither the cat **nor** the dog is here* (single negation)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Subject-verb agreement**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Neither the cat **nor** the dog is missing.* (verb singular; closer subject)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Neither you **nor** I am wrong.* (verb agrees with "I": am)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Rule**: Verb agrees with nearest subject; formal speakers sometimes use plural (*neither...nor are*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Parallel structure**: Required (*neither X nor Y*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **3. both...and** (inclusive; both elements included)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Function**: Join two things/ideas; emphasize inclusion of both
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Structure**: **both** [element 1] **and** [element 2]
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Both the teacher **and** the student agreed.* (both parties)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She is both intelligent **and** beautiful.* (possesses both qualities)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *Both reading **and** writing are essential.* (both skills needed)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Subject-verb agreement**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Both Mary **and** John are here.* (verb plural; "both...and" always plural)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Rule**: "Both...and" always triggers plural verb
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Emphasis**: "Both...and" emphasizes inclusivity more than simple "and"
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She likes coffee **and** tea.* (neutral)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She likes both coffee **and** tea.* (emphatic; definitely both)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Position**: "Both" begins, "and" connects elements
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **4. not only...but also** (addition with emphasis; positive addition)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Function**: Add second element with emphasis; suggest exceeding expectations
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Structure**: **not only** [element 1], **but** **also** [element 2]
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Element 1 is true, AND element 2 is additionally true (and may be surprising)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Not only did she pass, **but** she also topped the class.* (exceeded expectation)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He is not only intelligent, **but** also kind.* (multiple positive traits)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *This solution is not only efficient, **but** also cost-effective.* (multiple benefits)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Emphasis vs. "and"**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She is intelligent and kind.* (factual)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She is not only intelligent, **but** also kind.* (emphasizes multiple positive traits)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Inversion with "not only"**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Not only did she pass, **but** she also topped.* âœ“ (inversion for emphasis)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She not only passed, **but** she also topped.* âœ“ (no inversion)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Note**: Fronted "not only" often triggers inversion (formal style)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Subject-verb agreement**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Not only the teacher but also the students were present.* (plural; "students" is closer)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Rule**: Agrees with nearest subject element
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Variations**: *not only...but* (without "also"), *not just...but also*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **5. whether...or** (alternative conditions; often with if-constructions)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Function**: Present alternative conditions; often used with "or not" for binary choice
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Structure**: **whether** [condition 1] **or** [condition 2]
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Whether you win **or** lose, play fair.* (both outcomes acceptable)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *I don't care whether she comes **or** not.* (indifference about outcome)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *Whether it rains **or** shines, we'll have the picnic.* (regardless of condition)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **"Whether or not" construction**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Whether or not you agree, the decision is final.* (regardless)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Whether you agree or not, the decision is final.* (same meaning, alternate order)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Distinction from "if"**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Whether* presents two genuine alternatives (both possible)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *If* suggests conditionality (one may not occur)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Examples**: *I don't know whether she'll come.* (either outcome possible) vs *If she comes, tell her...* (only if it happens)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Formal/Literary register**: More common in formal writing
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **6. as...as** (comparison/equality, some uses correlative)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Function**: Express equality or similarity; "to the same degree/extent"
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Structure**: [element] **as** [adjective/adverb] **as** [element]
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Examples**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She is as intelligent **as** her sister.* (equal intelligence)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He runs as fast **as** a cheetah.* (equality of speed)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *She is twice **as** tall **as** him.* (comparative degree)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Intensification with "not so...as"**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She is not as intelligent **as** he is.* (not equal)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He is not so tall **as** she is.* (formal; not as tall)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Comparative vs. Equality**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *as...as* = equality (same degree)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *-er...than* = comparative (different degrees)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Position**: Symmetrical around adjective/adverb
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **7. Parallel Structure Requirement (All Correlative Pairs)**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Rule**: Elements joined must be grammatically equal
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Examples of violations**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *Either to study **or** working* (infinitive + gerund)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *Either to study **or** to work* (parallel infinitives)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *Both she and him are happy* (nominative + objective pronouns)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *Both she **and** he are happy* (parallel nominative pronouns)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Exam focus**: Identify parallel structure violations and correct them
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **8. Exam Strategy for Correlative Conjunctions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Identify the pair**: Determine which correlative conjunction is used
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Check parallelism**: Ensure both coordinated elements are grammatically equal
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Verify subject-verb agreement**: With paired conjunctions, verb typically agrees with nearest subject
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Recognize emphasis**: Correlatives provide more emphasis than simple coordinating conjunctions
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Common error**: Missing parallel structure; wrong case form for pronouns
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **C. Subordinating Conjunctions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Definition**: Words that join a dependent clause to an independent clause
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Function**: Introduce dependent/subordinate clauses; establish relationship between clauses
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Number**: Large open class; many subordinating conjunctions available
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚ **Position**: Usually before dependent clause (can follow main clause in some cases)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **1. Temporal Subordinating Conjunctions** (WHEN - time relationships)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Before**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Before she arrived, I cleaned the house.* (prior time)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He studied before the exam began.* (earlier action)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Meaning**: The dependent action occurs first; independent action follows
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **b) After**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *After she arrived, we had dinner.* (subsequent time)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He left after the meeting ended.* (following action)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Meaning**: The dependent action occurs first; then independent action follows
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **c) When**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *When she called, I was sleeping.* (simultaneous/concurrent)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *I knew when he arrived.* (moment of realization)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Can indicate simultaneous action or specific moment
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Tense interaction**: Often paired with past continuous or simple past
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **d) While**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *While she was studying, he was sleeping.* (simultaneous ongoing actions)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *I worked while she played.* (contrast of concurrent actions)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Emphasizes simultaneity and often contrast
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Usage**: Typically indicates overlapping time periods
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **e) Until/Till**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *I studied until I passed.* (continuing until achievement)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Don't leave until I tell you.* (wait for signal)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Action continues up to a point, then stops
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Common mistake**: *until not* vs *unless* distinction
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **f) As soon as**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *As soon as she called, I rushed over.* (immediate sequence)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Let me know as soon as you finish.* (moment of completion)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Meaning**: Emphasizes immediacy; one action follows immediately after another
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **g) Whenever**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Whenever he called, I answered.* (habitual/every time)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Come by whenever you're free.* (any time is suitable)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Meaning**: Indicates repeated action or general time flexibility
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **h) Since**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *Since I graduated, I've lived here.* (starting point to now)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *It's been five years since I saw him.* (elapsed time)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Meaning**: Starting point of duration; often with perfect tenses
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **2. Causal Subordinating Conjunctions** (WHY - reason/cause)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Because**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He failed because he didn't study.* (direct cause)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Because it was raining, we stayed inside.* (fronted, indicates cause)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Most direct causal relationship; "for the reason that"
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Most common**: Most frequent causal conjunction
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **b) As**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *As you already know, I'm leaving.* (causal/explanatory)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *As we were late, the film had started.* (reason for situation)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Multiple meanings**: Can be temporal, causal, or comparative
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Causal usage**: More formal than "because"
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **c) Since**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Since you're here, let's begin.* (causal: "given that")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Since nobody objected, we proceeded.* (because/seeing that)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Dual function**: Can be temporal (elapsed time) or causal (reason)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Distinction from "because"**: "Since" somewhat formal; weaker causal force than "because"
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **d) Now that**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *Now that you mention it, I agree.* (given this new information)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *Now that he's gone, we can relax.* (since he is no longer here)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Meaning**: Indicates reasoning based on changed circumstances
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **3. Conditional Subordinating Conjunctions** (IF - condition/hypothesis)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) If**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Factual condition**: *If you study, you'll pass.* (possible future)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Contrary-to-fact**: *If I were rich, I'd travel.* (hypothetical; counterfactual)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Introduces condition; consequence depends on meeting condition
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Tense patterns**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *If + present, will + verb* (real future condition)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *If + past subjunctive, would + verb* (hypothetical/unreal)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *If + past perfect, would have + verb* (past counterfactual)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Position**: Can precede or follow main clause
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **b) Unless**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Unless you study, you'll fail.* (negative condition; "except if")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *I won't go unless she comes.* (dependent on her coming)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Negative condition; "if not"
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Equivalence**: *Unless X* â‰ˆ *If not X*
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Common mistake**: Using negative in both clauses (*Unless she doesn't come* âœ—)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **c) Provided (that) / Providing (that)**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *I'll help, provided you pay me.* (formal; on the condition that)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Providing she agrees, we'll start tomorrow.* (alternative form)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Register**: More formal than "if"; emphasizes stipulation
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **d) As long as / So long as**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *As long as you try, I'm satisfied.* (continuous condition)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *I'll stay, so long as you want me here.* (ongoing stipulation)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Meaning**: Condition must remain true; emphasizes duration
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **e) In case**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *Take an umbrella in case it rains.* (precaution; "if it should happen")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *Keep this number in case you need help.* (preparation for possibility)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Meaning**: Anticipates possible condition; preparation focus
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **4. Concessive/Adversative Subordinating Conjunctions** (DESPITE - exception/contrast)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Although / Though**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Although she was tired, she continued working.* (despite tired state)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Though he was sick, he attended.* (conceding the contradiction)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Concedes contradictory information; "in spite of the fact that"
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Equivalence**: Both similar; "though" slightly more informal/literary
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Position**: Can appear at beginning or middle of sentence
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **b) Even though / Even if**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Even though he tried, he failed.* (strong concession; despite obvious effort)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Even if he tried, he'd still fail.* (hypothetical concession)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Distinction**: *Even though* acknowledges actual fact; *Even if* acknowledges possibility
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **c) While**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *While I agree with you, I have concerns.* (concessive use; temporal vs adversative)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Dual function**: Can be temporal or adversative
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Meaning (adversative)**: "Although," acknowledging contrary viewpoint
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **d) Whereas**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Whereas traditional methods are slow, digital tools are fast.* (contrast)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Usage**: Often at beginning of formal contrast
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Register**: Formal; often used in legal/academic writing
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **e) Yet**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *He studied hard, yet he failed.* (coordinating use; see earlier)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Note**: "Yet" primarily coordinating conjunction; rare subordinating use
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **5. Comparative Subordinating Conjunctions** (AS/THAN - comparison)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Than**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She is taller than I am.* (comparative degree)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He works more efficiently than she does.* (comparative manner/degree)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Establishes comparison; introduces second element of comparison
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Case after "than"**:
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She is taller than I [am].* (nominative; subject of implied clause)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *I like her more than [I like] him.* (objective; object of implied clause)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Test**: Complete the implied clause to determine case
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Ellipsis**: Often omits repeated verbs
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **b) As**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *She is as intelligent as her brother.* (equality; see Correlative section)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Note**: Often appears as part of "as...as" correlative structure
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **6. Purpose Subordinating Conjunctions** (SO THAT - goal/intention)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) So that**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *She studied hard so that she could pass.* (express goal)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *We left early so that we wouldn't be late.* (achieve result)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Meaning**: Introduces clause expressing purpose/goal
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Common in formal writing**: Academic, technical contexts
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **b) In order that / So as to** (infinitive alternative)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *He exercised in order that he might stay healthy.* (formal; purpose)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *She spoke clearly so as to be understood.* (infinitive form; often preferred)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Register**: "So as to" more common in modern English
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **7. Result Subordinating Conjunctions** (SUCH/SO THAT - consequence)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) So...that**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *It was so hot that we couldn't work.* (result of degree)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He spoke so softly that nobody heard.* (extent/degree leading to result)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Pattern**: Intensifier (*so*) + adjective/adverb + *that* + result clause
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Meaning**: Indicates cause-effect through degree
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **b) Such...that**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *It was such beautiful weather that we went outside.* (quality/type leading to result)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Pattern**: *such* + noun + *that* + result
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Distinction**: *So...that* with adjectives/adverbs; *Such...that* with nouns
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **8. Subordinating Conjunction Placement & Punctuation**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **a) Initial position (dependent clause first)**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Because he studied, he passed.* (comma after dependent clause)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Rule**: Comma required when dependent clause precedes independent
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *If you study, you'll succeed.* (conditional; comma required)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **b) Final position (dependent clause last)**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *He passed because he studied.* (no comma typically)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Rule**: Comma often omitted when dependent clause follows
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ *He succeeded if he studied.* (no comma)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **c) Mid-sentence positioning** (rare)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ *He passed, because he studied hard, with flying colors.* (awkward)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Generally avoided**: Breaks clause unity
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **9. Exam Strategy for Subordinating Conjunctions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Identify the relationship**: Temporal, Causal, Conditional, Concessive, Comparative, Purpose, Result
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Tense matching**: Ensure appropriate tense sequence with conditional/temporal conjunctions
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Punctuation**: Add comma when dependent clause precedes independent
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â”œâ”€â”€ **Common error**: Using wrong conjunction type (e.g., using "because" for temporal relationship)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚       â””â”€â”€ **Subordination vs. Coordination**: Recognize when to use subordinate vs. coordinate conjunctions
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **D. Exam Strategy & Key Testing Points**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **1. Conjunction Type Identification**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *She worked hard **and** she succeeded* (coordinating: addition)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *She worked hard, **and** she succeeded* (same; just with comma for clarity)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *She succeeded **because** she worked hard* (subordinating: causal)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Skill**: Distinguish coordinating vs. subordinating relationships
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **2. Comma Rules with Conjunctions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *She studied hard but she failed* (comma required with coord. conjunction)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *She studied hard, but she failed* (correct)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *If he comes please tell me* (comma required after dependent clause)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *If he comes, please tell me* (correct)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Rule**: Comma required with coordinating conjunction between independent clauses; comma required when subordinating conjunction begins sentence
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **3. Parallel Structure with Correlative Pairs**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *She likes either reading **or** to play games* (infinitive + gerund)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *She likes either reading **or** playing games* (both gerunds)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **Principle**: Coordinated elements must be grammatically parallel
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Common error**: Mixing verb forms, noun types, voice
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **4. Subject-Verb Agreement with Conjunctions**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Subject1 **and** Subject2* = Plural verb (*Both are here*)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Subject1 **or** Subject2* = Verb agrees with nearest subject
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *Neither Subject1 **nor** Subject2* = Verb agrees with nearest subject
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Exam focus**: Apply correct agreement rules based on conjunction type
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **5. Coordinating vs. Subordinating**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *Although he was tired, he continued.* (subordinating; expresses contrast)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ“ *He was tired, yet he continued.* (coordinating; expresses contrast)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ âœ— *Although he was tired, yet he continued.* (mixing both; redundant)
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â””â”€â”€ **Rule**: Use either subordinating or coordinating; don't combine (avoid "although...but")
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ **6. Tense Sequence with Conditionals**
â”‚       â”‚   â”‚   â”‚   â”œâ”€â”€ *If + present, will + base* = Future real (*If you study, you will pass*)
